<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p><i>Aquila rapax rapax</i>
</p>
</td></tr>

</tbody></table>
<p>The <b>tawny eagle</b> (<i>Aquila rapax</i>) is a large, long-lived <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a>. Like all <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, it belongs to the family <i><a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a></i>.  Its heavily feathered legs illustrate it to be a member of the subfamily <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">Aquilinae</a>, also known as “booted eagles”.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles have an extensive but discontinuous breeding range that constitutes much of the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> continent as well as the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, with rare residency occurring in the southern <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. <sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  Throughout its <a href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Range (biology)">range</a>, it favours open dry <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Semidesert" class="mw-redirect" title="Semidesert">semideserts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a> <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> plains. Despite its preference for areas of <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">aridity</a>, the species seldom occurs in areas where trees are entirely absent.<sup id="cite_ref-Hustler_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It is a resident breeder which lays one to three <a href="/wiki/Bird_egg" title="Bird egg">eggs</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">stick nest</a> mostly commonly in the crown of a tree.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>  The tawny eagle is perhaps the most highly opportunistic of all of its taxonomic clan, and often scavenges on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> or engages in <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a> towards other carnivorous animals but is also a bold and active predator, often of relatively large and diverse prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  It is estimated that tawny eagles can reach the age of 16 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, precipitous declines have been detected throughout the tawny eagle’s range. Numerous factors, particularly loss of nesting habitat due to <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Persecution" title="Persecution">persecution</a> (largely via <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisoning</a>) and other <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic mortality</a> (largely through contact with various manmade objects) are driving the once numerous tawny eagle perhaps to the brink of extinction.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dutch naturalist <a href="/wiki/Coenraad_Jacob_Temminck" title="Coenraad Jacob Temminck">Coenraad Jacob Temminck</a> described the tawny eagle in 1828 from an Indian <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">type specimen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> "Tawny eagle" has been designated the official name by the <a href="/wiki/International_Ornithologists%27_Union" title="International Ornithologists&#39; Union">International Ornithologists' Union</a> (IOC).<sup id="cite_ref-ioclist_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioclist-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <i>Aquila</i> is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for “eagle” while <i>rapax</i> is also Latin for “to grasp”, and relates to the word rapacious, i.e. highly predatory.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown2_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagle</a> subfamily within the <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a> family.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The booted eagle clan are <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a> and study of <a href="/wiki/Karyotype" title="Karyotype">karyotypes</a> has indicated that they likely have few to no close external relations within the overall extant accipitrid family.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The booted eagle subfamily all have feathers covering their legs and are distributed in every continent that contains accipitrids.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> has been traditionally defined as largish, dark-hued and long-winged eagles of open country.<sup id="cite_ref-Valli_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valli-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> However, study of <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> have resulted in several reclassifications. These include the moving of smaller, paler and more forest-dwelling eagles in <i>Aquila</i>, the separation of the unique <a href="/wiki/Clanga_(genus)" title="Clanga (genus)">spotted eagles</a> to the genus <i>Clanga</i> as well as the division of some small eagles to the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hieraeetus" class="mw-redirect" title="Hieraeetus">Hieraeetus</a></i> outside of <i>Aquila</i>. Furthermore, genetic research has further revealed a schism in superficially similar eagles between the tawny eagle and its close relatives and other superficially similar <i>Aquila</i> such as the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>) <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a>. To date, the tawny eagle species group and golden eagle species group are still contained in the same genus despite the apparent lack of close relation.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Valli_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valli-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg/220px-Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg/330px-Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg/440px-Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg 2x" data-file-width="722" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raubadler_Aquila_rapax.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle sitting on the ground displaying typical features: a mid-sized eagle with a longish neck, well-feathered legs, well-proportioned frame and large bill lacking a deep gape.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle was previously treated as <a href="/wiki/Biological_specificity" title="Biological specificity">conspecific</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagle</a> (<i>Aquila nipalensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> These eagles were considered part of the same species as recently as 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The steppe and tawny eagles were split based on pronounced differences in morphology and anatomy. The steppe eagle is a larger bird, with a much more pronounced <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">gape</a>, and differs by appearance in its blockier frame, bigger wings and evidences different coloring at all stages of development, despite some morphs of the two eagles vaguely resembling one another.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olson_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the respective species differ in ecology (dietary biology, nesting habits) and are strongly <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric</a> in their breeding ground distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Two molecular studies, each based on a very small number of genes, indicate that the species are distinct, but disagree over how closely related they are.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Genetically, the tawny eagle may cluster more closely with the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">imperial eagle</a> <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a> despite the steppe eagle being more so <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatric</a> with those northerly Eurasian eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(8409160315).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tawny_Eagle_%288409160315%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_%288409160315%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tawny_Eagle_%288409160315%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_%288409160315%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tawny_Eagle_%288409160315%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_%288409160315%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1043" data-file-height="1053" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(8409160315).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The distinctly darker subspecies native to India and elsewhere in Asia, <i>A. r. vindhiana</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>There are three described races of tawny eagles. The subspecific classification of the species has at times been considered complicated by variations and existence of different morphs, in turn they were once considered tentative.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, each subspecies is largely allopatric in geography, the only primary ambiguities lying in the northern part of <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east Africa</a> where both African races may intergrade.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>A. r. rapax</i>; distributed in Africa from the southern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> and central <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> to all points southwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, this race may range up to as far north as <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> as well (thus likely intergrading considerably with the following subspecies which is widespread in Ethiopia).<sup id="cite_ref-Ash_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Adult often of this nominate subspecies are often relatively more strongly rufous in colour than other races and are sometimes dark streaked below. Meanwhile, the juvenile tends to be light rufous. <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> lengths in this race have been measured at 485 to 540&#160;mm (19.1 to 21.3&#160;in) in males and 509 to 565&#160;mm (20.0 to 22.2&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The mean wing chord length in two samples of <i>A. r. rapax</i> measured 501 and 512&#160;mm (19.7 and 20.2&#160;in) in males and 541 and 545&#160;mm (21.3 and 21.5&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Musindo_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musindo-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The tail length of both sexes in <i>A. r. rapax</i> measures 245 to 295&#160;mm (9.6 to 11.6&#160;in) with a <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tarsus</a> length of 79 to 92&#160;mm (3.1 to 3.6&#160;in). Body mass can range roughly from 1.6 to 3.1&#160;kg (3.5 to 6.8&#160;lb) in overall samples of at least 36 eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><i>A. r. belisarius</i>; this race resides in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">west Africa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and southwest <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> as well as far south as the northern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clouet_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clouet-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This race is described to appear “neater” than the nominate subspecies, possibly due to this race often occurring in even more desert-like conditions than the other races and thus having more compact feathering.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It is when compared to the nominate, often duller and browner above, showing less of a rufous tinge. Meanwhile, individual pale morph of <i>A. r. belisarius</i> often a shade or two darker than pale nominate but not consistently so.  Although said to be slightly larger, measurement data shows this subspecies to of broadly very similar size to the nominate race. In males, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> is 495 to 535&#160;mm (19.5 to 21.1&#160;in) and, in females, the wing chord is 500 to 562&#160;mm (19.7 to 22.1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The mean wing chord lengths were reportedly 515&#160;mm (20.3&#160;in) in males and 525&#160;mm (20.7&#160;in), which indicates a slightly less pronounced sexual dimorphism than in the nominate race.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As for body mass, 1 male was found to weigh 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb) while three females weighed from 1.9 to 2.5&#160;kg (4.2 to 5.5&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><i>A. r. vindhiana</i>; excluding the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, this race likely comprises all the tawny eagles found in Asia, such as in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> However, ambiguities exists on where the range of <i>A. r. belisarius</i> ends and of <i>vindhiana</i> begins, especially in <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle Eastern</a> areas.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes <i>A. r. vindhiana</i> is suggested as full species.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies averages darker than either other race and usually is lacking in warmer rufous tone. In general, it is somewhat more similar in hue to the steppe eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Adult irises are sometimes brown in <i>A. r. vindhiana</i> (again reminiscent of the steppe eagle). The pale morph of this race is greyer and less rufous than African tawny eagles, although generally juveniles and immature are sometimes more rufous. It may be marginally the smallest subspecies, although in general the tawny eagle evidences remarkably little size variation across its wide range. <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">Wing chord</a> measurements are 473 to 535&#160;mm (18.6 to 21.1&#160;in) in males and 510 to 560&#160;mm (20 to 22&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baker_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In males, the wing chord reportedly averages about 495&#160;mm (19.5&#160;in) and in females, it averages 525&#160;mm (20.7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In males the tail length is 242 to 258&#160;mm (9.5 to 10.2&#160;in) and in female it is 242 to 285&#160;mm (9.5 to 11.2&#160;in). The <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tarsus</a> length of male <i>A. r. vindhiana</i> is 80 to 87&#160;mm (3.1 to 3.4&#160;in) and in females is 84 to 91&#160;mm (3.3 to 3.6&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baker_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Unsexed adults in India weighed from 1.5 to 2.1&#160;kg (3.3 to 4.6&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg/220px-2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg/330px-2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg/440px-2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1271" data-file-height="847" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2011-tawny-eagle-closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close-up showing gape extending only to below the middle of the eye</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle is considered to appear "inelegant, scruffy-looking" but has a fairly characteristic aquiline silhouette.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The species has a fairly long neck and long deep bill with a gape line level with the eye, moderately long wings with fairly pronounced “fingers” and a slightly rounded to almost square-ended and shortish tail, which can be more reminiscent of the tail of a vulture than that of other eagles. The feathering on the legs is extensive and can appear almost baggy-looking.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AliS_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AliS-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  The bill and head are strong and bold, the body well-proportioned and feet are powerful while the countenance is quite fierce-looking.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> While perching, the tawny eagle tends to sit rather upright, often on stumps, posts, low trees or treetops for long periods of the day or may descend to the ground to walk somewhat unsteadily with a more horizontal posture. The wingtips when perched are roughly even with the tip of the tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Adults have variably colored eyes, ranging from yellow to pale brown to yellow brown, while those of juveniles are dark brown. Both the cere and feet are yellow at all ages.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle is <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphic</a> with considerable individual variation in <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a>, resulting in occasional disparities in plumages that can engender confusion in some.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In adulthood, they can vary in coloration from all dark grey-brown to an occasionally streaky (or more plain) foxy-rufous to buffish-yellow.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shirihai_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirihai-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Most adults are usually a general grey-brown or rufous-tawny color, with occasional pale spotting visible at close quarters on the nape and belly, coverts uniformly toned as the body.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The nape is consistently dark and uniform despite the feathers often being tipped paler with other feathers in adults, lacking the contrasting paler feathers often seen in other <i>Aquila</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Females, in addition to being slightly larger, may tend to be slightly darker and more streaked than the males.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The most blackish-brown individuals tend to occur in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Adults often show relatively little varying colors apart from their somewhat blacker wing and tail feathers, though when freshly molted great wing coverts and secondaries may show small pale tips which may form pale lines along closed wing has tawny upper parts and blackish flight feathers and tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The head is often similarly tawny in colour as the body but may also sometimes shows darker eyebrows, other thin brown streaks or a darker chin.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the tail is plain or obscurely dark barred (with around 7 subtle bands). The dark morph adult is essentially all dark, dull brown. Some dark morph tawny eagles with wear may show irregular streaking or molting browns and more blackish feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Intermediate morph are dark to <a href="/wiki/Rufous" title="Rufous">rufous</a> brown above with the mantle and wing coverts variably streaked or molted lighter rufous as is the head with the crown or crown-sides being paler. The intermediate morph's underside is largely rufous (especially farther south in Africa) with breast and flanks very heavily and broadly streaked dark brown, though at times appears all dark brown contrasting with plain trousers and crissum.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Pale morph adult tawny eagles always show a clear contrast between the pale body and wing coverts which bear darker flight feathers and tail. In pale morphs, the underparts are rufous buff to lighty tawny-brown, phasing into somewhat darker lesser and median wing coverts to darker brown to even blackish greater coverts and flight feathers. The head may too be tawny in pale morph tawny eagles but sometimes with thin brown streaks or darker chin. Below pale morph adults are all light rufous to tawny buff or brown, sometimes paler below the belly area. In worn individuals the bodily feathers of pale morph tawny eagles can appear almost whitish.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  Dark morph juvenile tawny eagles are generally light rufous to rufous brown with creamier lower back to upper tail coverts. Juveniles show thinly pale-tipped dark brown greater coverts and remiges while the tail is barred grey and brown usually with a narrow creamy tip. Dark morph juveniles may fade to pale buff or creamy often before molting into browner plumage. Subsequent stages are not as well-known but it appears dark morph subadults gradually manifest a darker brown or rufous brown color on the mantle, as well as on the head and upper breast while maintaining a buffish rear body (i.e. lower back and rump patch). Generally other morphs are similar but not as well-known and are perhaps individually inconsistent. Many are rufous or sandy after a molt but have mottling later on, the extent of pale feathers indicative perhaps of the their ultimate adult morph.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_eagle_(Aquila_rapax_rapax)_streaked_morph.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Tawny_eagle_%28Aquila_rapax_rapax%29_streaked_morph.jpg/220px-Tawny_eagle_%28Aquila_rapax_rapax%29_streaked_morph.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Tawny_eagle_%28Aquila_rapax_rapax%29_streaked_morph.jpg/330px-Tawny_eagle_%28Aquila_rapax_rapax%29_streaked_morph.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Tawny_eagle_%28Aquila_rapax_rapax%29_streaked_morph.jpg/440px-Tawny_eagle_%28Aquila_rapax_rapax%29_streaked_morph.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1804" data-file-height="2705" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_eagle_(Aquila_rapax_rapax)_streaked_morph.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A streaked morph of the nominate subspecies of tawny eagle.</div></div></div>
<p>In flight, the tawny eagle appears as a large raptor with a noticeably protruding head on a long neck, with a deep chest, long and broad wings with a somewhat narrower seven-fingered hand.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The trailing edge of the wing is slightly curved outwards, indenting at the junction of primaries and secondaries, whilst the rounded, medium-length tail is usually held spread.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The deep beats of the kinked wings can make their flight appear rather heavy and slow but they are quicker and more expansive in wing movements and often less forceful-looking than larger <i>Aquila</i> like steppe eagles and can be very agile when chasing other raptors to rob them.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles soar with flat wings or very slightly raised and hands only slightly lower, and may fly similarly in a glide but may too arch when in a fast glide.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Adult dark morphs are more or less uniform dark brown above and below, showing indistinctly and slightly paler and greyish primaries on both sides. Above, the main contrast on dark morphs above is paler creamy rump patch while, on the underside, the greyish color is contrasted with blackish tips and a diffused trailing edge along both the wings and tail. Intermediate morph tawny eagles are variably rufous streaked on brown to rufous brown on the back and wing coverts with a similar contrasting pale rump above as dark morphs. Below the intermediate’s heavy dark streaks are only subtly different and their coloring can appear almost uniform. The wing quills of intermediate morphs are often greyer with a stronger contrast of the paler inner primaries and blackish wing ends. Pale morph are all pale tawny or buffish on both sides of the wing, which contrasting strongly with demarcated dark brown about the greater coverts, flight feathers and tail and usually the scapulars. The primaries are quite pale on pale morphs with sometimes the hint of a pale carpal comma. Some pale adults have pale bases to all the underprimaries and the quills are sometimes unbarred, but more usually the feathers have dense but narrow dark bars.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Dark morph juveniles are light rufous to pale tawny body above which contrasts strongly with dark brown greater coverts, rear scapulars, flight feathers and tail, in turn all highlighting the creamy lower back to tail coverts. Below dark morph juveniles can look similar to pale morph adults apart from trailing whitish edges and often irregular pale diagonals along tips of greater wing coverts, though usually these fade early on. Little is known plumage development but the young eagles <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">moult</a> into brown, becoming patchy with intermediate often showing 1-3 darker bars on wing linings.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The underparts of subadults (i.e. around 2 to 3 years or old) are typically two-toned, with darker brown about the breasts, belly and underwings coverts while the remainder of the underbody is creamy light in colour. This two-toned pattern is evidenced in subadult tawny eagles both from India and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Adult plumage is obtained between the 4th and 5th years of life.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_(33280280216).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_%2833280280216%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_%2833280280216%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_%2833280280216%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_%2833280280216%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_%2833280280216%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_%2833280280216%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="3874" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_(33280280216).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A richly rufous-colored tawny eagle.</div></div></div>
<p>This is a large bird of prey, though is medium-sized for an <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a> and it is one of the smaller species in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i>. Among currently accepted species in the genus, it is of quite similar size to <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's eagles</a> (<i>Aquila fasciata</i>) (though is notably longer winged), slightly larger than <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagles</a> (<i>Aquila spilogaster</i>) and much larger than <a href="/wiki/Cassin%27s_hawk-eagle" title="Cassin&#39;s hawk-eagle">Cassin's hawk-eagles</a> (<i>Aquila africanus</i>). Otherwise, females of the larger species of <i>Aquila</i> are frequently around twice as heavy as an average tawny eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> As is typical in birds of prey, the female tawny eagle is larger than the male, though relatively modestly so and a difference between the sexes is typically up to 15%.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In total length, tawny eagles can measure from 58 to 75&#160;cm (23 to 30&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A typical length for a tawny eagle is considered about 65&#160;cm (26&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Wingspans can measure from 157 to 190&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 2&#160;in to 6&#160;ft 3&#160;in). Weight can range in fully grown birds from 1.5 to 3.1&#160;kg (3.3 to 6.8&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ARKive2011_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARKive2011-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Average weights were reported in one study as 1.91&#160;kg (4.2&#160;lb) in 5 males and 1.97&#160;kg (4.3&#160;lb) in 5 females.<sup id="cite_ref-Musindo_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Musindo-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In another study, 10 unsexed adult tawny eagles were found to have weighed 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb) on average while, for the same data pool, a sample of 15 had an average wingspan of 182.9&#160;cm (6&#160;ft 0&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Mendelsohn_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mendelsohn-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Another small sample of African males, sample size four, averaged 1.85&#160;kg (4.1&#160;lb) while three females averaged 2.28&#160;kg (5.0&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The mean mass of the species in one estimate was 2.3&#160;kg (5.1&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In all standard measurements combined, the wing chord can vary from 473 to 565&#160;mm (18.6 to 22.2&#160;in), the tail from 242 to 295&#160;mm (9.5 to 11.6&#160;in) and the tarsus from 79 to 92&#160;mm (3.1 to 3.6&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>The <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">culmen</a> length of Kenyan tawny eagles was measured at 33.3 to 42.4&#160;mm (1.31 to 1.67&#160;in), averaging 37.7&#160;mm (1.48&#160;in), while the gape width is 44&#160;mm (1.7&#160;in) on average, ranging from 40.3 to 49.9&#160;mm (1.59 to 1.96&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The hallux-claw, the enlarged rear talon often used as a killing instrument on accipitrids, can measure from 27 to 37.7&#160;mm (1.06 to 1.48&#160;in), averaging 31.8&#160;mm (1.25&#160;in) in one sample and 32.3&#160;mm (1.27&#160;in) in another. The talon size is not especially large for a booted eagle and is proportionately similar in size to those of steppe eagles and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagles</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confusion_species">Confusion species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Confusion species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2572" data-file-height="1715" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_Aquila_rapax.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The slightly darker northern and central African subspecies, <i>A. r. belisarius</i>, in flight displaying the species typical flight profile.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle lives in multiple areas where other broadly similar brownish hued and largish raptors often occur. Thus identification can be said to seldom be straightforward. <sup id="cite_ref-Kemp2_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp2-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> One source that can especially engender potential confusion in its wintering range is the formerly conspecific <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagle</a>. The steppe is larger with a shorter neck, relatively longer and narrower wings, a more massive beak, particularly via the exceptional depth of the gape (although in flight can appear smaller headed due its less protruding neck) and has a longer and rounder tail. Furthermore, steppe eagles tends to have much bolder and widely spaced barring on the wings than tawny eagles and more distinct dark trailing wing edges and paler throats.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shirihai_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirihai-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Beyond steppe eagles, comparisons to various other groups of sympatric booted eagles may be made. Compared to the <a href="/wiki/Clanga_(genus)" title="Clanga (genus)">spotted eagles</a>, in tawny eagles, the tail is longer, the bill more prominent, the wings usually less squared-off in appearance, the neck longer and a rangier overall look is evidenced despite these species being of often similar size. In contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">imperial eagles</a>, the wings of the tawny eagle are broader and have less even trailing edges, the bill is slightly less prominent and the wings are more likely to be held slightly upwards while the body size is smaller. When compared to the golden eagle species complex, of which only the golden and the <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila verreauxii</i>) are usually relevant (albeit three dissimilar and sympatric pale-bellied eagles, of a size with tawny eagles or smaller, are found as part of this evolutionary chain), the size of the tawny eagle is considerably smaller, the wings do not taper as do they nor are as likely to be held in a strong dihedral and proportionately, the bill is notably longer and the tail is rather shorter.<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">Greater spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>), like the steppe eagle a <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a> breeding eagle who often winters near the residencies of tawny eagles, is fairly similar but that species has a relatively shorter and broader tail, less baggy feathers on the legs and is usually rather darker and more uniform in adult plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The <i>fulvescens</i> form of the greater spotted eagle must be distinguished from the pale forms of the tawny eagle via their underwing pattern, often with completely blackish underwing coverts and usually plain looking dark remiges over the entire primaries with more distinct pale carpal arcs.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davidson_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The also migratory <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>) is smaller than the tawny eagle and more compact with a distinct white U above the tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davidson_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The residential African <a href="/wiki/Wahlberg%27s_eagle" title="Wahlberg&#39;s eagle">Wahlberg's eagle</a> (<i>Hieraeetus wahlbergi</i>) can have a similar uniform plumage as in tawny eagles but always has greyer flight feathers and is much smaller than tawny eagles with relatively longer and more rectangular wings and a longer, narrower and straighter ended tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davidson_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> in juvenile plumage can appear similar to the pale and intermediate morph tawny eagles but the imperial eagle is usually visibly larger, with slenderer, longer wings, a longer, broader tail as well as having dark brown streaking on the chest, mantle and wing coverts and bearing more distinct pale trailing edges and wing bars.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shirihai_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirihai-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Dark morph tawny eagles in India may be distinguished from similarly-sized <a href="/wiki/Black_eagle" title="Black eagle">black eagles</a> (<i>Ictinaetus malaiensis</i>) by the latter being slenderer with longer, darker and more paddle-shaped wings with a narrower base and having a much longer, narrower and distinctly barred tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> More dissimilar eagles such as <a href="/wiki/Circaetinae" title="Circaetinae">Circaetinae</a>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Brown_snake_eagle" title="Brown snake eagle">brown snake eagles</a> (<i>Circaetus cinereus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black-breasted_snake_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Black-breasted snake eagle">black-breasted snake eagles</a> (<i>Circaetus pectoralis</i>) and juvenile <a href="/wiki/Bateleur" title="Bateleur">bateleurs</a> (<i>Terathopius ecaudatus</i>), are sometimes mentioned as a potential source of confusion but are usually rather distinct (all larger headed, rather smaller billed, shorter tailed and bare legged with often less uniform coloring) even in their most similar hues.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davidson_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calls">Calls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Calls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tawny eagles are generally silent in most of their range.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, unlike steppe eagles, which are extremely silent away from their breeding grounds, they are said to occasionally vocalize in any season.<sup id="cite_ref-Shirihai_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirihai-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> It is also more vocal when not breeding than the spotted eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The usual call is a rather harsh, hollow-sounding and loud bark, variously transcribed as <i>kowk-kowk</i>, <i>kau-kau</i>, <i>kiok-kiok</i>, <i>ki-ark</i> and so on. The call is fairly high-pitched (slightly less deep than the steppe eagle’s when the latter is breeding) but is still deeper voiced than spotted eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a> it is said the call is loud and far-travelling.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Male tawny eagles are the most frequent vocalizers in the species, particularly during sky-dances, but also in other contexts. These include but are not limited to food arguments, disturbances during nesting and by males attracting females for food passes.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In nine years of monitoring tawny eagles in <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>,  however, the call was not heard once.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The reasons for its silence may be due to the flat landscape in which it inhabits.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Other call recorded include a harsh grating <i>k eke ke</i>… in aerial courtship displays and a throaty <i>kra</i> in kleptoparastic pursuits. A <i>kra-kra</i> call may emitted at times to warn intruders.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The female may also emit an occasional mewing, high <i>shreep-shreep</i> at the nest as well as a rare raucous scream (possible food-begging and alarm-calls, respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davison_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davison-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The young chick tawny eagle chips initially but once its feathers emerge, it tends to beg with a loud call, i.e. <i>we-yik, wee-yik</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Tawny eagles have an extremely extensive natural distribution. The African population can be found in three, fairly discrete populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> One of these is found in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> in south-central <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, possibly northern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, southwestern <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senegambia" title="Senegambia">Senegambia</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, central and southern <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> eastward through southern <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, northern and central <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> to most of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> (but for the northeast and central-east).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ash_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The north African population is scarce. In Morocco, they are heavily depleted with a few populations left in some regions such as <a href="/wiki/Tarfaya" title="Tarfaya">Tarfaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tan-Tan" title="Tan-Tan">Tan-Tan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Souss-Massa" title="Souss-Massa">Souss-Massa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thevenot_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thevenot-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> They are likely <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpated</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, where they were once frequent.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, some tawny eagles occur in <a href="/wiki/Gambia" class="mw-redirect" title="Gambia">Gambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and (though possibly not breeding) in <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett-Lemaire_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-Lemaire-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cheke_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheke-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">central Africa</a>, the tawny eagle is found in central and eastern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> and throughout the drier portions of <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and in the entire nations of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> (quite often residing in the <a href="/wiki/Luangwa_River" title="Luangwa River">Luangwa valley</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chambeshi_River" title="Chambeshi River">Chambeshi drainage</a>), <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>. In east Africa, it is considered perhaps the most widely distributed and regularly sighted brown eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, the tawny eagle is found throughout <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> (now often rare apart from <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chipinge_Safari_Area" title="Chipinge Safari Area">Chipinga Uplands</a>), <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> (still regular in <a href="/wiki/Okavango_Delta" title="Okavango Delta">Okavango Delta</a>) and some areas of <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, southern and western <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cuando_Cubango_Province" title="Cuando Cubango Province">Cuando Cubango</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cunene_Province" title="Cunene Province"> Cunene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hu%C3%ADla_Province" title="Huíla Province">Huíla</a> <a href="/wiki/Namibe_Province" title="Namibe Province">Namibe</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Malanje_Province" title="Malanje Province">Malanje</a>), <a href="/wiki/Eswatini" title="Eswatini">Eswatini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a> and northern and central parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, i.e. mainly north of the <a href="/wiki/Orange_River" title="Orange River">Orange River</a> but sometimes down to the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penry_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penry-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barnes_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle may be extinct as a breeder in <a href="/wiki/Swaziland" class="mw-redirect" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a> where it was last confirmed to have bred in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Eagle,_Pokaran,_district_Jaisalmer,_Rajasthan,_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Steppe_Eagle%2C_Pokaran%2C_district_Jaisalmer%2C_Rajasthan%2C_India.jpg/220px-Steppe_Eagle%2C_Pokaran%2C_district_Jaisalmer%2C_Rajasthan%2C_India.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Steppe_Eagle%2C_Pokaran%2C_district_Jaisalmer%2C_Rajasthan%2C_India.jpg/330px-Steppe_Eagle%2C_Pokaran%2C_district_Jaisalmer%2C_Rajasthan%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Steppe_Eagle%2C_Pokaran%2C_district_Jaisalmer%2C_Rajasthan%2C_India.jpg/440px-Steppe_Eagle%2C_Pokaran%2C_district_Jaisalmer%2C_Rajasthan%2C_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="493" data-file-height="416" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Steppe_Eagle,_Pokaran,_district_Jaisalmer,_Rajasthan,_India.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle perched in <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> in India.</div></div></div>
<p>Out of Africa, the species may possibly be found in the southwestern part of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, i.e. in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and extreme southwestern <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tihamah" title="Tihamah">Tihamah</a> and <a href="/wiki/%27Asir_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="&#39;Asir Region">'Asir Regions</a>, but few to none confirmed breeding events have been reported in the last few decades.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle is considered a rare vagrant in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, though some are verified, other reports of them often turn out to be misidentified steppe eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> They are also known as a rare vagrant in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In Asia, the tawny eagle exists in isolation in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (as in Arabia, verified recent breeding is not known) and somewhat more continuously in eastern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (often in the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus valley</a>),  much of <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">north</a> and peninsular <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, eastward scarcely through southern <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Though Nepali tawny eagles are rarely recorded, it is thought that the species still resides there in lowland semi-deserts.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The Indian range is from <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Indo-Gangetic Plain</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan Plateau</a> with range continuing down to <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a> and (mainly north-central) <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Records of vagrating tawny eagles turning up in <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> are thought to have been likely misidentified steppe eagles or are based on now unidentifiable specimens.<sup id="cite_ref-Duckworth_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duckworth-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> A small handful of vagrants have been verified to turn up in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> (the only known appearance by an <i>Aquila</i> eagle there).<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Old reports of vagrancy, probably in need of confirmation, are known also from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bushveld.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Bushveld.jpg/220px-Bushveld.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Bushveld.jpg/330px-Bushveld.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Bushveld.jpg/440px-Bushveld.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bushveld.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tawny eagles are often at home in the arid and yet rich <a href="/wiki/Veld" title="Veld">bushveld</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Tawny eagles occurs in fairly open country at varied elevations but usually live in <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">drier areas</a>. In <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, the species breeds often in relatively moist <a href="/wiki/Forest-savanna_mosaic" title="Forest-savanna mosaic">forest-savanna mosaics</a> but can move into <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_dry_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical dry broadleaf forests">dry woodlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-deserts</a> when not breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, the species prefers <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">forested areas</a> near <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a> with adjacent <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a>. Elsewhere in Africa, tawny eagles typically inhabit <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">wooded savanna</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">dry <i>Acacia</i> savanna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-desert</a> to <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert areas</a>. However, extreme desert areas, completely lacking in arborescent growth, are avoided nearly as much so as humid <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a>. It also occurs at times in manmade areas such as <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable lands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shoulder_(road)" title="Shoulder (road)">roadsides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_land" title="Agricultural land">farmland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">cattle pastures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Professional_hunter" title="Professional hunter">game areas</a> if feeding opportunities occur in them.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ash_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Penry_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penry-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veld" title="Veld">thornveld</a> is often the preferred habitat with the tawny eagles mostly preferring stands of <i><a href="/wiki/Acacia" title="Acacia">Acacia</a></i>. Despite similar climates, within the <a href="/wiki/Miombo" title="Miombo">miombo woodland</a>, the tawny eagle tends to be more scarce.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In India, similar habitats may largely be used but the tawny eagle may fairly often occur too in the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">villages</a> and cultivations and frequents <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">garbage dumps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouses</a> somewhat more so than they do in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In addition to all gradients of arid zones, in India, the tawny eagle frequently is found around <a href="/wiki/Thorn_forest" title="Thorn forest">thorn forests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles may live from sea level to about 3,000&#160;m (9,800&#160;ft) but tends to prefer somewhat lower elevations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Despite a certain level of aridity expected in tawny eagle habitats, they normally will not nest unless a habitat meets certain demands.<sup id="cite_ref-Hustler_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle’s presence is predicated on the availability of <a href="/wiki/Ephemerality" title="Ephemerality">ephemeral</a> <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rainfall</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">wet season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hustler_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hustler2_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler2-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herholdt_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herholdt-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> This reliance on some rainfall is probably key to habitat quality and resulting prey populations to some extent, but also to the availability of nesting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hustler2_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler2-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle is by and large an obligate tree nester and so areas that become too arid to support tree growth or where trees are <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overharvested</a> are unlikely to retain the species.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_1.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_1.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_1.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="531" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle in flight.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle, quite unlike the steppe eagle, is largely sedentary and <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">non-migratory</a>. However, in Africa it is at times considered to be fairly <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> and can engage in some seasonal movements.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay2_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay2-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">west Africa</a>, <i>A. r. belisarius</i> rather regularly travels shorter distance to damp woodlands during October through November, returning north in April, and perhaps at least at times, migrates into <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalahari desert">Kalahari region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a> and may vagrate to southern <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett-Lemaire_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-Lemaire-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes, tawny eagles seem to ping semi-regularly between Ethiopia and west Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay2_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay2-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Some long distance wandering has even been reported, such as a vagrant <i>A. r. belisarius</i> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and as far as <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (where twice recorded in the 1950s) and even <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> (3 winter records in the 1990s) and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> From the Indian range, individuals vagrate not infrequently to nearby <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, most likely as juveniles post-dispersal wanderings, but reports of the species wandering into <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">southeast Asia</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> are now considered likely apocryphal.<sup id="cite_ref-Duckworth_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duckworth-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Generally, in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern Africa</a>, tawny eagles usually seldom seem to leave their established breeding territories and juvenile eagles generally wander no more than several dozen kilometres from their original nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> A bird banded as a nestling in <a href="/wiki/Esigodini" title="Esigodini">Esigodini</a> was recovered quite nearby at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Rixon" title="Fort Rixon">Fort Rixon</a> more than two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> However, in a rather far dispersal for southern Africa, one eagle banded as a nestling was recovered 330&#160;km (210&#160;mi) away from its nest of origin in Zimbabwe four years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Dowsett_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dowsett-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Inconsistent and seemingly unpredictable movements by tawny eagles have been proven via experimental ecological studies to be actually be instances of eagles searching out new areas to compensate for lack of rainfall.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann2_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann2-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> While non-breeding steppe eagles are often slightly <a href="/wiki/Aggregation_(ethology)" title="Aggregation (ethology)">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flocking_(behavior)" title="Flocking (behavior)">flock</a> at opportunistic feeding sources, the tawny eagle is usually considered solitary.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> However, groups of two to three tawny eagles are sometimes seen, such as in the Indian subcontinent, but occasionally group sizes may even exceed this figure.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-22" class="reference"><a
