href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Mirpur_Division" title="Mirpur Division">Mirpur Division</a> of <a href="/wiki/Azad_Kashmir" title="Azad Kashmir">Azad Kashmir</a> in Pakistan, small flocks of tawny eagles have reportedly been witnessed gathering in warmer spots between November and February, over three years of study.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Small groups or aggregations are known to occur in Africa as well near concentrated foods and even communal roost have been reported in trees, power pylons or on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Like many large raptors, the tawny eagle probably spends the majority of its day perched but take wing a few times a day.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Unlike most large eagles, in India at least, tawny eagles are often fairly accustomed to humans and may allow fairly close approach by observers.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg/220px-Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg/330px-Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg/440px-Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax_-Ethiopia_-with_roadkill-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>With a <a href="/wiki/Black-backed_jackal" title="Black-backed jackal">black-backed jackal</a>, road traffic victim in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle is unique as an <i>Aquila</i> eagle in the lack of apparent specialization in its feeding behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  While most other <i>Aquila</i> will opportunistically scavenge, the tawny eagle freely takes to <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenging</a> on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>, perhaps doing so at all times of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Routine scavenging often drives tawny eagles to <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">refuse dumps</a> in or near <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">villages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouses</a>, particularly in India, and to associate quite often with <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">vultures</a> at carrion sites. They are also frequently recorded on roadsides where <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">roadkill</a> provides a steady food source.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> More routinely than almost any other raptor, perhaps, it is a very skilled <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">pirate</a>, regularly engaging in kleptoparasitism of other <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>  However, descriptions of the tawny eagle as “sluggish”, “not very distinguished” and “unimpressive” are not particularly apt as the tawny eagle is a highly rapacious predator that attacks very variable ranges of live prey including particularly large prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This species hunts mainly by a short dive or pounce from a perch or by stoop from up high in a soaring flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In the Indian subcontinent, preferred hunting perch trees were <i><a href="/wiki/Vachellia_nilotica" title="Vachellia nilotica">Vachellia nilotica</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Prosopis_cineraria" title="Prosopis cineraria">Prosopis cineraria</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Capparis_decidua" title="Capparis decidua">Capparis decidua</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It may also often forage by walking on the ground. Mostly, tawny eagles target live prey that is on the ground, seldom targeting <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">arboreal</a> prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> However, they will sometimes fly down and take birds on the wing.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Avian prey known to be killed in the air has ranged from <a href="/wiki/Speckled_pigeon" title="Speckled pigeon">speckled pigeons</a> (<i>Columba guinea</i>) to <a href="/wiki/Flamingo" title="Flamingo">flamingoes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles may hunt frequently in pairs during the breeding season, often securing larger prey than is does in the non-breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Sometimes this <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">tandem hunting</a> by pairs can occur in any season.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is likely that tandem hunting by pairs involves one bird engaging in conspicuous flight to distract the quarry while another flies inconspicuously to sneak up on and kill the prey, as has been reported in other tandem hunting raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">Nocturnal animals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Genet_(animal)" title="Genet (animal)">genets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Springhare" class="mw-redirect" title="Springhare">springhares</a> have been preyed upon by tawny eagles in areas where there was no possibility they were killed by traffic at night. In addition to other observations have been made where tawny eagles drink and bath at night, some nocturnal subsistence behaviour by this species has been inferred but no irrefutable evidence has been brought forth either.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Semi-regular attendance at <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">grassfires</a> in India, presumably in order to capture displaced creatures, has been reported.<sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> More than 200 species, including both live prey and carrion, are known to be eaten by tawny eagles and they may have one of the most variable diet of all tropical eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vernon_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vernon-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Reportedly, most prey the tawny eagle will take alive will weigh not less than 125&#160;g (4.4&#160;oz) and not more than 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb), however live prey has been revealed to be regularly more variable than even that estimate represents.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> One compilation study showed that, compared to 8 other <i>Aquila</i> and spotted eagles, the tawny eagle’s diet was the most evenly spread across all weight classes of prey from under 63&#160;g (2.2&#160;oz) to over 4&#160;kg (8.8&#160;lb), though took prey in the latter prey class slightly less so than the much larger <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wedge-tailed_eagle" title="Wedge-tailed eagle">wedge-tailed eagles</a> (<i>Aquila audax</i>). This study further determine that the most often focused on weight class in tawny eagle’s diets were 0.5 to 1&#160;kg (1.1 to 2.2&#160;lb) and 1 to 2&#160;kg (2.2 to 4.4&#160;lb) prey class, accounting for a little less than half of the prey by quantity.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Probable_live_prey">Probable live prey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Probable live prey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_(Varanus_albigularis)_(16517371167).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_%28Varanus_albigularis%29_%2816517371167%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_%28Varanus_albigularis%29_%2816517371167%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_%28Varanus_albigularis%29_%2816517371167%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_%28Varanus_albigularis%29_%2816517371167%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_%28Varanus_albigularis%29_%2816517371167%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_%28Varanus_albigularis%29_%2816517371167%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="3648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_juvenile_eating_a_White-throated_Monitor_Lizard_(Varanus_albigularis)_(16517371167).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle with its prey, a <a href="/wiki/Rock_monitor" title="Rock monitor">rock monitor</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Determining whether prey has been taken alive at the nests of tawny eagles is generally considered to be difficult, although observations suggest that during breeding tawny eagles usually deliver fresh prey while raising young, indicating that such prey are usually either taken alive or newly pirated from other predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Within a Kenyan study, only 1.9% of prey brought to tawny eagle nests was thought likely to be from carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> While hunting prey, it often takes a variety of prey usually focusing somewhat on small to medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, usually medium-sized to large <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and occasionally medium to large-sized <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> A diet analysis in <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> indicated that among 160 prey items, 36.9% were mammals, 51.9% were birds, 10% were reptiles and 1.2% were amphibians.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The leading prey species here were <a href="/wiki/Helmeted_guineafowl" title="Helmeted guineafowl">helmeted guineafowl</a> (<i>Numida meleagris</i>), at about 21% of the prey total, and <a href="/wiki/Scrub_hare" title="Scrub hare">scrub hare</a> (<i>Lepus saxatilis</i>), at about 16% of the total.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> A similar dietary study conducted in <a href="/wiki/Lochinvar_National_Park" title="Lochinvar National Park">Lochinvar National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> found a higher proportion of birds and amphibians (61.4% &amp; 5.5% respectively), with a surprisingly number of <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> being taken, largely the <a href="/wiki/Red-knobbed_coot" title="Red-knobbed coot">red-knobbed coot</a> (<i>Fulica cristata</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/African_pygmy_goose" title="African pygmy goose">African pygmy goose</a> (<i>Nettapus auritus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>  The variation in diet between the preceding two study sites is due to differences in habitat and prey availability.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> A very detailed study was conducted in <a href="/wiki/Tsavo_East_National_Park" title="Tsavo East National Park">Tsavo East National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, of the diet of the local tawny eagles over different years. Of 543 total prey items, 41.2% were mammals, 35.4% were birds, and 23.4% were reptiles and amphibians. Of these the most important prey was <a href="/wiki/Kirk%27s_dik-dik" title="Kirk&#39;s dik-dik">Kirk's dik-dik</a> (<i>Madoqua kirkii</i>) at 21.7% of the total, <a href="/wiki/Yellow-necked_spurfowl" title="Yellow-necked spurfowl">yellow-necked spurfowl</a> (<i>Pternistis leucoscepus</i>) at 6.8% of the total, <a href="/wiki/Cape_hare" title="Cape hare">cape hare</a> (<i>Lepus capensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Crested_francolin" title="Crested francolin">crested francolin</a> (<i>Dendroperdix sephaena</i>) both at 6.3% and <a href="/wiki/Red-crested_korhaan" title="Red-crested korhaan">red-crested korhaan</a> (<i>Lophotis ruficrista</i>) at 5.9%; additionally, unidentified <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> constituted 21.3% of the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The dik-dik is a small <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a> and is much larger than a tawny eagle. The tawny eagle certainly took dik-diks weighing up to 4&#160;kg (8.8&#160;lb) and possibly even 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb), with this eagle taking about 80 dik-diks in Tsavo East each year.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In different areas of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, small dietary studies determined the diet at nests to be highly variable. In the <a href="/wiki/Highveld" title="Highveld">Highveld</a>, about 52% of 60 prey items were mammals, 45% were birds and a small amount were <a href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">fish</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Timbavati_Game_Reserve" title="Timbavati Game Reserve">Timbavati</a> and <a href="/wiki/Klaserie_Game_Reserve" title="Klaserie Game Reserve">Klaserie</a>, 63% of the diet was birds, 34% of it was mammals and 3% were reptiles. At Highveld, 25% of the diet consisted of <a href="/wiki/Yellow_mongoose" title="Yellow mongoose">yellow mongoose</a> (<i>Cynictis penicillata</i>), 15% <a href="/wiki/Cape_ground_squirrel" title="Cape ground squirrel">Cape ground squirrels</a> (<i>Xerus inauris</i>) and 13% <a href="/wiki/Helmeted_guineafowl" title="Helmeted guineafowl">helmeted guineafowl</a>. At Timbavati and Klaserie, various <a href="/wiki/Francolin" title="Francolin">francolins</a> were strongly predominant in foods, at about 44% with another 17% by various <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongooses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Out of the southern and eastern areas of Africa, less quantitative analysis has been undertaken into the feeding habits of tawny eagles, even around nests. What is known of their prey elsewhere is mainly from wide-ranging surveys, secondary accounts and photographs.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It appears in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> that the tawny eagle may have a close predatory relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_grass_rat" title="Abyssinian grass rat">Abyssinian grass rat</a> (<i>Arvicanthis abyssinicus</i>) while one tawny eagle there tore open the nest of the <a href="/wiki/Stresemann%27s_bushcrow" title="Stresemann&#39;s bushcrow">Stresemann's bushcrow</a> (<i>Zavattariornis stresemanni</i>) to access the prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ash_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Unidentified large rats constituted a great majority of prey delivered during the nestling growth stage at some east African nests.<sup id="cite_ref-VanSomeren_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanSomeren-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Fewer details are known about the prey of tawny eagles in the Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> One study, without quantitative data known, listed the prey of tawny eagles in <a href="/wiki/Saurashtra_(region)" title="Saurashtra (region)">Saurashtra</a> as <a href="/wiki/Indian_palm_squirrel" title="Indian palm squirrel">Indian palm squirrels</a> (<i>Funambulus palmarum</i>), eggs of <a href="/wiki/Red-wattled_lapwing" title="Red-wattled lapwing">red-wattled lapwings</a> (<i>Vanellus indicus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongooses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_hare" title="Indian hare">Indian hares</a> (<i>Lepus nigricollis</i>) and even <a href="/wiki/Bengal_fox" title="Bengal fox">Bengal fox</a> (<i>Vulpes bengalensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, the prey incidentally reported in India is extremely varied, including even the hindlegs of a <a href="/wiki/Jungle_cat" title="Jungle cat">jungle cat</a> (<i>Felis chaus</i>) (but this may have been scavenged).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In general, tawny eagles in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">south Asia</a> may focus on less varying prey in general, often reportedly favoring desert-dwelling <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a> and hares.<sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fitzwater_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzwater-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>  Tawny eagles may hunt a couple dozen species of <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodent</a> from different parts of the range, ranging in size from the 30&#160;g (1.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Natal_multimammate_mouse" title="Natal multimammate mouse">Natal multimammate mouse</a> (<i>Mastomys natalensis</i>) to the 3.6&#160;kg (7.9&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/South_African_springhare" title="South African springhare">South African springhare</a> (<i>Pedestes capensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzwater_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzwater-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rock_hyrax" title="Rock hyrax">Rock hyrax</a> (<i>Procavia capensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Yellow-spotted_rock_hyrax" title="Yellow-spotted rock hyrax">yellow-spotted rock hyrax</a> (<i>Heterohyrax brucei</i>) are occasionally preyed upon by tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Kingdon_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle may be one of the most accomplished predators of mongoose, many food studies reflecting relatively high numbers of them and they appear to be one of the most feared predators at <a href="/wiki/Meerkat" title="Meerkat">meerkat</a> (<i>Suricata suricatta</i>) colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Ash_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Irwin_74-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irwin-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Karoo</a>, the local tawny eagles reportedly live mostly off of mongooses, either meerkats or yellow mongooses.<sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton2_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton2-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Similarly small or slightly larger <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivores</a> like <a href="/wiki/Striped_polecat" title="Striped polecat">striped polecats</a> (<i>Ictonyx striatus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Genet_(animal)" title="Genet (animal)">genets</a> are not infrequently prey for tawny eagles as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bat-eared_fox" title="Bat-eared fox">Bat-eared foxes</a> (<i>Otocyon megalotis</i>) may too be taken alive at times.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Various <a href="/wiki/Monkey" title="Monkey">monkeys</a> may be eaten, although not infrequently as carrion, tawny eagles may too in seldom cases attack juveniles of monkeys such as <a href="/wiki/Patas_monkey" title="Patas monkey">Patas monkeys</a> (<i>Erythrocebus patas</i>), <a href="/wiki/Grivet" title="Grivet">grivets</a> (<i>Chlorocebus aethiops</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Vervet_monkey" title="Vervet monkey">vervet monkeys</a> (<i>Chlorocebus pygerythrus</i>) up to the size of juveniles of several species of <a href="/wiki/Baboon" title="Baboon">baboon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kingdon_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kingdon-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> However, unlike with <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">larger eagles</a>, the troops of <a href="/wiki/Hamadryas_baboon" title="Hamadryas baboon">certain baboons</a> do not seem to regard tawny eagles as a threat based on their behavioural responses.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> While most ungulate prey other than dik-diks is probably largely scavenged as carrion or stolen from other predators, the small calves of ungulates such as <a href="/wiki/Thomson%27s_gazelle" title="Thomson&#39;s gazelle">Thomson's gazelle</a> (<i>Eudorcas thomsonii</i>) are sometimes apparently killed by tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> A general picture appears to emerge that tawny eagles quite often takes relatively large mammalian prey, surprisingly often creatures weighing up to 3 to 4&#160;kg (6.6 to 8.8&#160;lb) such as hares, dik-diks, the young of other antelopes, hyraxes and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg/220px-Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="85" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg/330px-Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg/440px-Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anagoria_Waffenkiebitz_Raubadler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle is mobbed by a <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith_lapwing" title="Blacksmith lapwing">blacksmith lapwing</a>; these eagles are a potential menace to many different kinds of birds.</div></div></div>
<p>While mammals prey varies from rodents to hares, mongooses and small antelopes, the diversity and size range of bird taken may be even more impressive and more than 120 avian prey species have been reported in the prey spectrum.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Included in the prey spectrum are various species of <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">ducks</a> and small or gosling <a href="/wiki/Geese" class="mw-redirect" title="Geese">geese</a><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Gamebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Francolin" title="Francolin">francolins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guineafowl" title="Guineafowl">guineafowl</a><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>, many <a href="/wiki/Dove" class="mw-redirect" title="Dove">doves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>, mostly medium-sized species of <a href="/wiki/Bustard" title="Bustard">bustard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hornbill" title="Hornbill">hornbill</a><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> and numerous <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> from small <a href="/wiki/Courser" title="Courser">coursers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lapwing" title="Lapwing">lapwings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rail_(bird)" title="Rail (bird)">rails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Little_grebe" title="Little grebe">grebes</a> to large <a href="/wiki/Flamingo" title="Flamingo">flamingoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">storks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a> both small and large.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Both young and adult Old World flamingoes of both African species are known to be attacked on occasion, as well as <a href="/wiki/White-breasted_cormorant" title="White-breasted cormorant">white-breasted cormorants</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax lucidus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Great_cormorant" title="Great cormorant">great cormorants</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax carbo</i>), all birds similar in size or somewhat heavier than the tawny eagle itself.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Even larger avian prey are taken including a <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">common crane</a> (<i>Grus grus</i>) killed by a pair in Saurashtra (although it was an injured one) and presumably adult female <a href="/wiki/Kori_bustard" title="Kori bustard">Kori bustard</a> (<i>Ardeotis kori</i>). If average-sized, these prey items likely weighed more than 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> More minor avian prey includes <a href="/wiki/Nightjar" title="Nightjar">nightjars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coucal" title="Coucal">coucals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandgrouse" title="Sandgrouse">sandgrouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asian_palm_swift" title="Asian palm swift">swifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue-tailed_bee-eater" title="Blue-tailed bee-eater">bee-eaters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pied_kingfisher" title="Pied kingfisher">kingfishers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coraciidae" title="Coraciidae">rollers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_wood_hoopoe" title="Green wood hoopoe">wood hoopoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grey_go-away-bird" title="Grey go-away-bird">turacos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> One small passerine the tawny eagle may routinely hunt is the super-abundant <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_quelea" title="Red-billed quelea">red-billed quelea</a> (<i>Quelea quelea</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Near <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">poultry farms</a>, tawny eagles can take to not infrequently lifting free-range <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chickens</a> (<i>Gallus gallus domesticus</i>) and other <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>, especially when the eagles must feed their young, thus in turn potentially drawing ire of local farmers.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AliS_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AliS-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various snakes are taken opportunistically by tawny eagles and they can be quite bold about hunting <a href="/wiki/Venomous_snake" title="Venomous snake">venomous snakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In southern Africa and Kenya the following snakes have been identified in the foods of tawny eagles: <a href="/wiki/Gongylophis_colubrinus" title="Gongylophis colubrinus">Egyptian sand boa</a> (<i>Gongylophis colubrinus</i>), young <a href="/wiki/African_rock_python" title="African rock python">African rock python</a> (<i>Python sebae</i>), <a href="/wiki/Psammophis" title="Psammophis">speckled sand racer</a> (<i>Psammophis punctulatus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Rufous_beaked_snake" title="Rufous beaked snake">rufous beaked snake</a> (<i>Rhamphiophis oxyrhynchus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black-necked_spitting_cobra" title="Black-necked spitting cobra">black-necked spitting cobra</a> (<i>Naja nigricollis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_mamba" title="Black mamba">black mamba</a> (<i>Dendroaspis polylepis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Boomslang" title="Boomslang">boomslang</a> (<i>Dispholidus typus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Puff_adder" title="Puff adder">puff adder</a> (<i>Bitis arietans</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown3_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown3-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> They also hunt <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> not infrequently given the chance, usually favoring fairly large species but capable of taking those ranging from <a href="/wiki/West_Indian_leopard_gecko" title="West Indian leopard gecko">geckos</a> to <a href="/wiki/Rock_monitor" title="Rock monitor">rock monitors</a> (<i>Varanus albigularis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> At one nest in Zimbabwe, <a href="/wiki/Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard">monitor lizards</a> made up 29% of 83 prey items, but they were only 8% of 107 prey items of 3 other nests in the same park.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> One of the most frequently seen prey to be taken by tawny eagles in India have been <a href="/wiki/Saara_hardwickii" title="Saara hardwickii">Indian spiny-tailed lizard</a> (<i>Saara hardwickii</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> More minor prey have included <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">toads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>. A tawny eagle in southern Africa was seen to wade into shallow water and successfully pull out a largish <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles can also take communal nesting and swarming insects fairly frequently. These are generally <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>, which can attract several of these eagles especially amongst non-breeding eagles and young ones. When visiting termites, the tawny eagles commonly eat <a href="/wiki/Alate" title="Alate">alates</a> and may, with an unusual lack of aggression, share the food source with several other birds of prey, including as many as a half dozen conspecifics.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> At times, tawny eagles can also be attracted to swarms of <a href="/wiki/Grasshopper" title="Grasshopper">grasshoppers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In one case, a tawny eagle was seen consuming the fruit of an <i><a href="/wiki/Adansonia" title="Adansonia">Adansonia</a></i> tree, an unusual instance of <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">frugivory</a> which is very rare in accipitrids other than one unusual species: the <a href="/wiki/Palm-nut_vulture" title="Palm-nut vulture">palm-nut vulture</a> (<i>Gypohierax angolensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A tawny eagle was once witnessed picking through <a href="/wiki/African_bush_elephant" title="African bush elephant">elephant</a> <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">dung</a> along with a <a href="/wiki/White-headed_vulture" title="White-headed vulture">vulture</a>, presumably searching for <a href="/wiki/Dung_beetle" title="Dung beetle">dung beetles</a> to consume.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carrion">Carrion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Carrion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_and_White-backed_Vultures_(Gyps_africanus)_(6001455023).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_White-backed_Vultures_%28Gyps_africanus%29_%286001455023%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_White-backed_Vultures_%28Gyps_africanus%29_%286001455023%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_White-backed_Vultures_%28Gyps_africanus%29_%286001455023%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_White-backed_Vultures_%28Gyps_africanus%29_%286001455023%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_White-backed_Vultures_%28Gyps_africanus%29_%286001455023%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_White-backed_Vultures_%28Gyps_africanus%29_%286001455023%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_and_White-backed_Vultures_(Gyps_africanus)_(6001455023).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle perched amongst <a href="/wiki/White-backed_vulture" title="White-backed vulture">white-backed vultures</a>, with which they are often obligated to share carrion in Africa.</div></div></div>
<p>Although the tawny eagle does hunt for food, it also relies extensively on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> as a food source.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although most booted eagles and <i>Aquila</i> will opportunistically feed on carrion, none is known to do so as routinely as the tawny eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> They have been recorded feeding on a huge array of carcasses as large as <a href="/wiki/African_bush_elephant" title="African bush elephant">African bush elephants</a> (<i>Loxodonta africana</i>) and at least as small as <a href="/wiki/Vervet_monkey" title="Vervet monkey">vervet monkeys</a> and perhaps even down to the size of a <a href="/wiki/Streptopelia" title="Streptopelia">dove</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp3_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp3-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps most frequently in Africa, tawny eagles will feed at carcasses of <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> At least 30-40 different species of ungulate have been recorded as carrion food-sources for these eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> At “vulture restaurants” in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, feeding stations with dead livestock meant to mitigate the rapid decline in population of most African vultures, the tawny eagle was the second most often recorded scavenger at just under 35% of 1088 of recorded birds to feed at them.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle shares its carrion food sources almost invariably with <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">vultures</a> and usually with several other scavengers such as <a href="/wiki/Jackal" title="Jackal">jackals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">hyenas</a>. Other birds that frequently also attend carrion are <a href="/wiki/Bateleur" title="Bateleur">bateleurs</a>, many other <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagles</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Marabou_stork" title="Marabou stork">marabou storks</a> (<i>Leptoptilos crumenifer</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dean_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dean-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The producer-scrounger theory predicts that vultures rely on eagles for information on carcasses.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogada_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogada-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Due to their smaller size, eagles , i.e. tawny eagles and bateleurs, are able to begin foraging earlier in the morning and are thus more likely to locate a carcass first.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogada_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogada-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In 91 observed carcass in southern Africa, tawny eagles were verified to be the first to find 5 of them. Furthermore, vultures usually arrived in less than 40 minutes (in 75% of cases) after the tawny eagles found the carcass.<sup id="cite_ref-CJ_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> At large carcasses, there is a hierarchical social structure based on the size of the scavenger.<sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Maasai_Mara" title="Maasai Mara">Maasai Mara</a>, the top scavengers were the considerably to slightly heavier mammals, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyenas</a> (<i>Crocuta croctua</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black-backed_jackal" title="Black-backed jackal">black-backed jackals</a> (<i>Canis mesomelas</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">feral dogs</a> (<i>Canis lupus familiaris</i>), then the <a href="/wiki/Lappet-faced_vulture" title="Lappet-faced vulture">lappet-faced vulture</a> (<i>Torgos tracheliotos</i>), the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCppell%27s_griffon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rüppell&#39;s griffon">Rüppell's griffon</a> (<i>Gyps rueppellii</i>), followed by all other vultures with the tawny eagle and the bateleur in the second most and the most subordinate scavenger positions.<sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Similar scavenger hierarchies have been reported elsewhere as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dean_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dean-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Bateleurs were the most likely to first find a carcass of the Maasai Mara scavengers and both the tawny and bateleur were considered as scavengers with “low competitive ability and high search efficiency”.<sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>However, tawny eagles will at times be able to displace the smaller species of vulture such as <a href="/wiki/Hooded_vulture" title="Hooded vulture">hooded vultures</a> (<i>Necrosyrtes monachus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_vulture" title="Egyptian vulture">Egyptian vultures</a> (<i>Neophron percnopterus</i>), both of which are similar in weight to the tawny eagles themselves, with one tawny eagle even reportedly keeping as many as 20 vultures at bay at a carcass. In general, based on the literature, such an event of aggressiveness by this species at a large carcass would surely be unusual.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles do tend to be dominant over <a href="/wiki/Bateleur" title="Bateleur">bateleur</a> at carcasses, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Gyps" title="Gyps">Gyps</a></i> or griffon vultures are usually the most numerous vultures in attendance at carrion and are considerably larger than tawny eagles but sometimes may briefly tolerate a tawny eagle to feed in their midst depending on the circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Houston_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp3_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp3-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Usually, the larger the group is of griffon vultures, the less likely the tawny eagle is to get to feed.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Houston_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp3_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp3-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The eagles not infrequently remain on the periphery of the vulture <a href="/wiki/Feeding_frenzy" title="Feeding frenzy">feeding frenzy</a> and wait for pieces of flesh to appear. Often they will be able to pick up small scraps but will wait until the carcass is finished and few vultures remain to feed. The tawny eagle can benefit from leading other scavengers to carrion or feeding subsequently to them since, unlike the largest and most aggressive vultures, such as <a href="/wiki/Lappet-faced_vulture" title="Lappet-faced vulture">lappet-faced vultures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinereous_vulture" title="Cinereous vulture">cinereous vultures</a> (<i>Aegypius monachus</i>), the tawny eagle cannot tear open large carcasses on their own and tend rely on another source to access any bits of the nutritious viscera.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Houston_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagles when finding an unopened large carcass have few feeding options although may eat the eyes in such circumstances, as was verified in the circumstance of a tawny eagle finding a <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horse</a> (<i>Equus ferus caballus</i>) carcass offered by researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-CJ_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">Roadkills</a> are another feeding option as they are often torn asunder by impact with automobiles and the eagle may be able to (at least briefly) monopolize the carcass.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps not coincidentally, in Maasai Mara, the tawny eagles were found to benefit from a carcass being nearer human habitations and in lower quality habitats relative to the other scavengers.<sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> In particularly in India, scavenging tawny eagles tend to regularly occur at <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfills</a> where vultures seldom come but wintering steppe eagles may often feed alongside them seasonally.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Garbage dumps are also visited in different parts of Africa such as <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> by hungry tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Semi-predaceous and aggressively disposed vultures, like <a href="/wiki/White-headed_vulture" title="White-headed vulture">white-headed vultures</a> (<i>Trigonoceps occipitalis</i>) in Africa and <a href="/wiki/Red-headed_vulture" title="Red-headed vulture">red-headed vultures</a> (<i>Sacrogyps calvus</i>) in India as well as the lappet-faced and cinereous vultures, tend to have little tolerance for tawny eagles, with the latter unlikely to approach until these aggressive vultures have had their fill.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, though, at times white-headed vultures and tawny eagles have been observed peaceably sharing roadkills in some instances.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Often tawny eagles will come to smaller carcasses of almost any animal, as will other smaller scavengers like bateleurs and hooded vultures as well as <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>, perhaps merely to avoid the competition that often occurs at large carcasses.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anderson_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CJ_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> One subadult tawny eagle was observed to be following a pack of <a href="/wiki/African_wild_dog" title="African wild dog">African wild dogs</a> (<i>Lycaon pictus</i>), almost certainly in order to scavenge off of their kills.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kleptoparasitism">Kleptoparasitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Kleptoparasitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_and_Young_Bateleur_(Terathopius_ecaudatus)_(6017682851).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_Young_Bateleur_%28Terathopius_ecaudatus%29_%286017682851%29.jpg/220px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_Young_Bateleur_%28Terathopius_ecaudatus%29_%286017682851%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_Young_Bateleur_%28Terathopius_ecaudatus%29_%286017682851%29.jpg/330px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_Young_Bateleur_%28Terathopius_ecaudatus%29_%286017682851%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_Young_Bateleur_%28Terathopius_ecaudatus%29_%286017682851%29.jpg/440px-Tawny_Eagle_%28Aquila_rapax%29_and_Young_Bateleur_%28Terathopius_ecaudatus%29_%286017682851%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tawny_Eagle_(Aquila_rapax)_and_Young_Bateleur_(Terathopius_ecaudatus)_(6017682851).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle perched with a young <a href="/wiki/Bateleur" title="Bateleur">bateleur</a>. The ecology of these species is often broadly similar but the tawny eagle usually dominates the bateleur in food competition.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle steals food from other raptors in addition to catching its own prey and coming to previously dead food sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Afrikaans" title="Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a> name for the tawny eagle is a "Roofarend", meaning the "<a href="/wiki/Robber" class="mw-redirect" title="Robber">Robber</a> Eagle".<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This behaviour is not entirely segregated from their scavenging on carrion behaviours but the considerable aggressiveness and boldness of the eagles in this circumstances are very different from their rather retiring disposition in scavenging contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> At times the tawny eagle is considered “fearless” in their piratical attacks and is certain to engage in them more frequently than almost any other member of the booted eagle clan or perhaps even birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Other related eagles like the steppe eagle and eastern imperial eagle, as well as most <a href="/wiki/Sea_eagle" title="Sea eagle">sea eagles</a>, can be locally regular kleptoparasites but tawny eagles rob prey from other birds with some regularity in every part of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Amongst all birds, only a few types of <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frigatebird" title="Frigatebird">frigatebirds</a> are likely to derive a majority of their subsistence from kleptoparasitism.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>  Generally, tawny eagles will surprise other birds of prey with a dashing stoop and yank away the prey item in a manner of seconds; they will seldom completely land if the prey item is intercepted on the ground so they can take off with the plundered item quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown3_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown3-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The size of birds that the tawny eagles have been known to pirate food away from have ranged from species as small as <a href="/wiki/Black-winged_kite" title="Black-winged kite">black-winged kites</a> (<i>Elanus caeruleus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrels</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>) to those as large as a <a href="/wiki/Bearded_vulture" title="Bearded vulture">lammergeier</a> (<i>Gypaetus barbatus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> There seems to few limits to the raptorial birds that the tawny eagle will not pirate from given the opportunity.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In one case, a pair of tawny eagles descended on a <a href="/wiki/Secretarybird" title="Secretarybird">secretarybird</a> (<i>Sagittarius serpentarius</i>) that had killed a large <a href="/wiki/Puff_adder" title="Puff adder">puff adder</a> and displaced both the secretarybird and an <a href="/wiki/African_harrier-hawk" title="African harrier-hawk">African harrier-hawk</a> (<i>Polyboroides typus</i>) that had tried to enter the fray, after which the eagle pair split the adder between them.<sup id="cite_ref-VanSomeren_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanSomeren-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Other raptors known to be attacked for piracy in well-known and often repeated instances have included <a href="/wiki/Dark_chanting_goshawk" title="Dark chanting goshawk">dark chanting goshawks</a> (<i>Melierax metabates</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bateleur" title="Bateleur">bateleurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanner_falcon" title="Lanner falcon">lanner falcons</a> (<i>Falco biarmicus</i>) and even the imposing <a href="/wiki/Martial_eagle" title="Martial eagle">martial eagles</a> (<i>Polemaetus bellicosus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagles</a>, the latter eagles having appeared to offer surprisingly little to no contest the tawny eagle’s piracy despite their great strength and formidable talons.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Carnivorous birds that are not traditionally considered raptorial birds, such as <a href="/wiki/Marabou_stork" title="Marabou stork">marabou storks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_ground_hornbill" title="Southern ground hornbill">southern ground hornbills</a> (<i>Bucorvus leadbeateri</i>), are also occasionally kleptoparasitized by tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Interspecific piracy may be most frequent on bateleur despite that species being similarly sized and powered as the tawny eagle. While 5 displacements of tawny eagles by bateleur were reported in a study on their interactions, 26 instances of tawny eagles displacing bateleurs were described, clearly far more.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson2_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Several smaller birds of prey were observed to be repeatedly robbed of their catches at a <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_quelea" title="Red-billed quelea">red-billed quelea</a> colony, including queleas crippled but not killed by lanner falcons, although some of the maimed queleas were contested by jackals as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On occasion, a tawny eagle will find itself on the losing end of a kleptoparasitic interaction. Somewhat larger eagles have been seen to displace tawny eagles off of prey. These include <a href="/wiki/African_fish_eagle" title="African fish eagle">African fish eagles</a> (<i>Haliaeetus vociferus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagles</a> and their cousins, <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> African fish eagles and <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_fish_eagle" title="Pallas&#39;s fish eagle">Pallas's fish eagles</a> (<i>Haliaeetus leucoryphus</i>) in India both seem to take precedence over tawny eagles at shared feeding sources such as carrion sites and <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water bird</a> nesting colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Bale_Mountains" title="Bale Mountains">Bale Mountains</a> of Ethiopia, <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> appear to engage in displacement of and may dominate the much smaller tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> As aforementioned, a bateleur can succeed in seldom instances in pirating tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson2_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Vultures, especially <a href="/wiki/Lappet-faced_vulture" title="Lappet-faced vulture">lappet-faced vultures</a>, may assert themselves at recent tawny eagle kills and certainly can displace the eagles in some circumstances; it is likely but not confirmed that <a href="/wiki/Jackal" title="Jackal">jackals</a> may too opportunistically rob eagles as they’ve been recorded doing with other eagles. Large kills, which can not infrequently include prey of up to twice the eagle’s own weight, are beyond the tawny eagle’s ability for <a href="/wiki/Wing_loading" title="Wing loading">wing loading</a>. Such kills are probably frequently lost to other carnivores.<sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown3_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown3-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In Ethiopia, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_wolves" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian wolves">Ethiopian wolves</a> (<i>Canis simensis</i>) were seen to rob tawny eagles repeatedly of freshly-caught rodents, succeeding in 5 of 21 attempts to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Even much smaller birds such as <a href="/wiki/House_crow" title="House crow">house crows</a> (<i>Corvus splendens</i>) have been seen to successfully rob a tawny eagle of its prey.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecific_predatory_relationship">Interspecific predatory relationship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interspecific predatory relationship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans,_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans%2C_India.jpg/220px-Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans%2C_India.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans%2C_India.jpg/330px-Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans%2C_India.jpg/440px-Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans%2C_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="491" data-file-height="470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_nipalensis_and_two_Milvus_migrans,_India.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle in India flies with two <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kites</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The tawny eagle occurrence in Africa and the Indian subcontinent places it in arguably two of the most competitive environments for birds of prey in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In turn, the tawny eagle seems to adapt via a lack of specialization on any particular prey type, hunting style or food source, and via including carrion in the diet quite often.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Many other raptors and eagles overlap in habitat use with tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> However, wintering and residential <i>Aquila</i> and spotted eagles that bear some relation usually use slightly differing habitats in contrast to the tawny eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tsavo_East_National_Park" title="Tsavo East National Park">Tsavo East National Park</a>, the ecology of this eagle was studied at length in contrast to bateleurs and much larger martial eagles, which can appear to have broadly similar habitat and prey preferences, as well as the slightly smaller but larger-clawed <a href="/wiki/African_hawk_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="African hawk eagle">African hawk eagles</a>, which tends to habituate to slightly more <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_dry_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical dry broadleaf forests">wooded dry areas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> In general within this study, all four eagle species derived a majority of their prey biomass from <a href="/wiki/Kirk%27s_dik-dik" title="Kirk&#39;s dik-dik">Kirk's dik-dik</a> but that the martial and African hawk-eagles tended to take slightly larger dik-diks than the bateleur and tawny eagles, took slightly more in the park per pair based on annual estimates and were more unlikely to scavenge the prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The diet of the tawny eagle and bateleur in Tsavo East overlapped by around 64%, whereas the diet of the tawny and martial eagles only overlapped by 29%. The tawny eagle was the only eagle here to heavily supplement their diet with alternate prey like snakes, although bateleurs also took a wide range of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Tsavo East study further indicated that the predatory pressure on dik-diks is mitigated temporally by the slightly staggered nesting seasons of each eagle, with bateleurs tending to nest rather earlier, the hawk-eagle slightly later, so the peak reliance on the prey did not generally overlap.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Further study has indicated that, in Africa, the bateleur broadly mirrors the tawny eagle in most respects of ecology.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> One stark difference from virtually any other known eagle is the tawny eagle’s nesting habits. That is that this eagle nests almost invariably on the top of the canopy of a tree, rather than a main trunk or large sturdy branch of trees (or on cliffs or, in steppe eagles, the ground).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bildstein_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bildstein-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The nesting location of tawny eagles runs more parallel to those of <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">vultures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mundy_156-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mundy-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Study in <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a> has shown that the tawny eagle and <a href="/wiki/White-backed_vulture" title="White-backed vulture">white-backed vulture</a> (<i>Gyps africanus</i>) will freely nest in the treetop nest built by the other species.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp3_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp3-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, other species, including large <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snake_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Snake eagle">snake eagles</a>, will use old nests built by tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although the habitats used by martial and tawny eagles have been reported as broadly similar, detailed study in the <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Karoo</a> found that the tawny species preferred areas with higher and more predictable summer rainfall and with higher <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">primary productivity</a> than the martial.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Opportunistically, the tawny eagle will sometimes prey upon smaller birds of prey but this is fairly infrequent and the capture of raptorial birds has thus far been seldom reported. A hungry or food-gathering male tawny eagle may infrequently plunder the nests of other raptorial birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VanSomeren_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VanSomeren-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Incautious, injured or distracted birds of prey may too be vulnerable to being killed as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Engel_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engel-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Diurnal birds of prey known to be preyed upon by tawny eagle in Africa have included <a href="/wiki/Black-winged_kite"
