title="Black-winged kite">black-winged kites</a>,  juvenile <a href="/wiki/Hooded_vulture" title="Hooded vulture">hooded vultures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pale_chanting_goshawk" title="Pale chanting goshawk">pale chanting goshawks</a> (<i>Melierax canorus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_falcon" title="Pygmy falcon">African pygmy falcons</a> (<i>Polihierax semitorquatus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tarboton_109-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarboton-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Engel_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engel-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In India, the tawny eagle has been known to prey upon <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harriers</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Shikra" title="Shikra">shikras</a> (<i>Accipiter badius</i>) and <a href="/wiki/White-eyed_buzzard" title="White-eyed buzzard">white-eyed buzzards</a> (<i>Butastur teesa</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ali_35-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Owls are apparently fairly vulnerable to tawny owl predation. Species that they’ve been known to prey on are <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>), <a href="/wiki/Spotted_eagle-owl" title="Spotted eagle-owl">spotted eagle-owls</a> (<i>Bubo africanus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owls</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>), <a href="/wiki/Pearl-spotted_owlet" title="Pearl-spotted owlet">pearl-spotted owlets</a> (<i>Glaucidium perlatum</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Marsh_owl" title="Marsh owl">marsh owls</a> (<i>Asio capensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Smeenk_47-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smeenk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Engel_183-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engel-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The fresh remains of a <a href="/wiki/Secretarybird" title="Secretarybird">secretarybird</a> were found in one tawny eagle nest in Africa but, if the eagles killed the bird rather than scavenged it, this would need confirmation.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The tawny eagle, despite being an eagle of intermediate size, does not seem to be subject to natural predators in adulthood as far as is known and can be said to fulfill the role of an <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Nests and young tawny eagle are commonly vulnerable to assorted natural predators but these are little known. A partial list of probable nest predators are likely <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivores</a> capable of climbing.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> One confirmed predator of nestling tawny eagles is the <a href="/wiki/Honey_badger" title="Honey badger">honey badger</a> (<i>Mellivora capensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_biology">Breeding biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Breeding biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pairing_and_Territories">Pairing and Territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pairing and Territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The tawny eagle often seems to pair for life. Like most birds of prey, they are quite <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorial</a> towards conspecifics.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The commonest display is single or mutual high circling or soaring often in wide spiral. Males will sometimes dive and stoop repeatedly around the female, though she does not usually respond by turning over.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Pairs may engage in the display each to strengthen pair bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally two tawny eagles will interlocks talons to descend rapidly, cartwheeling down 30 m or more within a few seconds, sometimes disengaging just before the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In other related eagles of the Aquilinae subfamily, cartwheeling interactions are usually considered to be aggressive fights between a territorial eagle and an intruder of the same gender. Prior studies thought this to be the case for the tawny eagle, with an estimated 82% of cartwheeling instances thought to be aggressive, 11% for courtship and 7% for apparent play. However, through closer observations evidence has been made of frequent cartwheeling between males and females as a regular part of the courtship display.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Undulating sky dances are sometimes performed too by males with a series of descents and upward swoops on partially close wings, accompanied by calling. However, instances of this seem to be rare.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In one instance, two males appeared to engage in a display for a single female.<sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_114-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Per one author’s opinion the aerial displays of the tawny eagle are “not particularly spectacular compared to other eagles”.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The breeding season tends to fall in March to August in northeastern Africa, October to June in west Africa and in almost all months of the year but in central, east and southern Africa, but mainly from May to November in Kenya and April to January in central and southern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vincent_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In India, the breeding season is usually November to May, but occasionally can vary from any time from October to August.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Mating generally occurs in and around the nest vicinity.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The density very variable on the African continent overall of breeding pair which were estimated to occupy about 75 to 300&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (29 to 116&#160;sq&#160;mi) each.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Zimbabwe nest spacing was found to be 7 to 10&#160;km (4.3 to 6.2&#160;mi) in one study. On the border of <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a>, 7 pairs found in a 460&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (180&#160;sq&#160;mi) area but in regular spaced pylon nests in western Transvaal, nests were 19 to 20&#160;km (12 to 12&#160;mi) apart.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hwange_National_Park" title="Hwange National Park">Hwange National Park</a>, over 11 years of study, 92 pairs on were found to be nesting over <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> in a 4,724&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1,824&#160;sq&#160;mi) area while 84 pairs on <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">Kalahari sands</a> in a 9,876&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3,813&#160;sq&#160;mi) area. Mean nest distances on basalt were around 4&#160;km (2.5&#160;mi) while on sands it was around 59&#160;km (37&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Hustler_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, the nesting density was considered high for the species at a pair per 28&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (11&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nest">Nest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The nests of the tawny eagle are large platforms, composed of sticks but sometimes incorporating animal bones.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Nesting sites tend to be open to the sky, in flat, open or hilly country, and offer a commanding a good view of the surrounding country. The sites are not infrequently close to <a href="/wiki/Depression_(geology)" title="Depression (geology)">watering holes</a> and, more so in India, close to villages.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Nests are usually 6 to 15&#160;m (20 to 49&#160;ft) above the ground, though seldom can be up to 30&#160;m (98&#160;ft) high.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Nests are located at the top crown of the tree and only very rarely are placed beneath the canopy or on a lateral branch.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, tawny eagles showed no nesting preference according to tree height or spatial distribution of trees;<sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> however, they preferred <i><a href="/wiki/Euphorbia" title="Euphorbia">Euphorbia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Boscia" title="Boscia">Boscia</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Euclea" title="Euclea">Euclea</a></i> tree species.<sup id="cite_ref-Kendall_160-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> In India, commonly used trees used in the northern areas are <i><a href="/wiki/Ficus_religiosa" title="Ficus religiosa">Ficus religiosa</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dalbergia_sissoo" title="Dalbergia sissoo">Dalbergia sissoo</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Mango" title="Mango">mango</a> trees while in the arid <a href="/wiki/Kutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Kutch">Kutch</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> areas they often nest in rather stunted <i><a href="/wiki/Vachellia_nilotica" title="Vachellia nilotica">Vachellia nilotica</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Prosopis_chilensis" title="Prosopis chilensis">Prosopis chilensis</a></i> (i.e. usually the nests here are 4 to 6&#160;m (13 to 20&#160;ft) high but are sometimes down to 4&#160;m (13&#160;ft) ).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Trees are usually selected that have prickly branches, presumably for protection.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Despite their prominent position in the trees, the nests can be surprisingly hard to perceive peering from the ground level.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vincent_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Kgalagadi_Transfrontier_Park" title="Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park">Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, tawny eagles build nests that are positioned in the canopy of large <i><a href="/wiki/Vachellia_erioloba" title="Vachellia erioloba">Vachellia erioloba</a></i> trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Herholdt_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herholdt-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> These Kgalagadi pairs tend to be the largest and tallest trees, averaging at 10.9&#160;m (36&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Herholdt_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herholdt-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles in India reportedly often nest in a tree over successive years, but the species is threatened by the lopping and cutting of all remaining suitable trees for fuel and fodder.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Tawny eagles in Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park, on the other hand, build new nests yearly and only 2% of nests are reused for breeding purposes the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-Herholdt_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herholdt-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Potential risk of collapse and growth of branches around the nest are thought to be factors limiting nest reuses in Africa. Usually new nests are not more than 2&#160;km (1.2&#160;mi) away from the prior nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Both sexes participate in nest building and the repair of a nest takes up to 4 to 7 weeks, though most of the construction can be completed within about a week.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> For an eagle, their nests are relatively wide, flat and shallow.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Nests may measure just under 1&#160;m (3.3&#160;ft) in diameter and 20&#160;cm (7.9&#160;in) deep but can easily reach over 1.3&#160;m (4.3&#160;ft) and 30&#160;cm (12&#160;in) with repeated uses.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Nests are usually lined with grass, leaves, seedpods and fur as well as odd objects such as newspapers, paper packets and polythene bags.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a>, tawny eagles have been recorded using nests of other species of <a href="/wiki/Raptor_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Raptor (bird)">raptor</a> such as <a href="/wiki/White-backed_vulture" title="White-backed vulture">white-backed vulture</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-headed_vulture" title="White-headed vulture">white-headed vulture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> At times, tawny eagles have been known to nest on top of the large communal nests of the <a href="/wiki/White-headed_buffalo_weaver" title="White-headed buffalo weaver">white-headed buffalo weaver</a> (<i>Dinemellia dinemelli</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In the Central <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Karoo</a> region of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, tawny eagles build their nests in large electric <a href="/wiki/Transmission_towers" class="mw-redirect" title="Transmission towers">transmission towers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Populations of large eagles like the <a href="/wiki/Martial_eagle" title="Martial eagle">martial eagle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagle</a> have been recorded breeding on these power pylons since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Between 2002 and 2003, 39% of electrical faults recorded on transmission lines were due to large eagle nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_191-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> As a result, problem nests were dismantled and rebuilt below the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_191-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs">Eggs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Eggs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg/220px-Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg/330px-Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg/440px-Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Aquila rapax</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg/220px-Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg/330px-Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg/440px-Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax_belisarius_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.91.2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Aquila rapax belisarius</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<p>Eggs are laid at intervals of several days, mainly timed to the dry season but at times also in the wet season.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Evidence from the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">Kalahari Desert</a> shows that egg-laying is timed to exploit a number of food resources with warmer weather in sync with young in the nest, such as various small mammals and the <a href="/wiki/Springbok" title="Springbok">springbok</a> (<i>Antidorcas marsupialis</i>) lambing season.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>  In India, intervals were more prolonged when the habitat was less optimal.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Of 26 tawny eagle nests monitored between 1988 - 1996 in the <a href="/wiki/Kgalagadi_Transfrontier_Park" title="Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park">Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park</a>, 84.6% of the laying dates occurred between May and June.<sup id="cite_ref-Herholdt_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herholdt-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> These laying dates are similar to populations in <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> <sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_Mara" title="Maasai Mara">Maasai Mara</a> region in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> as well as elsewhere in southern and southeastern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kendall2_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kendall2-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Cases of eggs being laid in southern Africa in July and August may be cases of replacement clutches.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Incidental data on laying phenology from north and West Africa shows the tawny eagles of the area lay eggs usually in the earlier part of the year, i.e. January to April; in Ghana egg-laying may range from October to February, though largely is in December-January; November-February egg-laying occurred in Ethiopia and apparently around April in Morocco.<sup id="cite_ref-Ash_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ash-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cheke_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheke-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thevenot_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thevenot-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Showing the variation in India, in <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, tawny eagles are mainly laying eggs in January while in <a href="/wiki/Kutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Kutch">Kutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jaisalmer" title="Jaisalmer">Jaisalmer</a>, the young are already leaving the nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Clutch sizes range from 1 to 3 eggs per nest, but average 1.7 eggs per clutch.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In drier years in <a href="/wiki/Hwange_National_Park" title="Hwange National Park">Hwange National Park</a>, clutch sizes appear to become reduced.<sup id="cite_ref-Hustler_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are white but variously and usually faintly marked with brown, varying from unmarked sometimes to quite well-marked with spots and blotches of reddish brown.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 67 eggs of the nominate subspecies, the eggs were 64 to 75.7&#160;mm (2.52 to 2.98&#160;in) in height by 49.9 to 60&#160;mm (1.96 to 2.36&#160;in) in diameter, with an average of 69.6&#160;mm ×&#160;54.8&#160;mm (2.74&#160;in ×&#160;2.16&#160;in) in the sample while another 30 from the same race averaged 71.5&#160;mm ×&#160;56.3&#160;mm (2.81&#160;in ×&#160;2.22&#160;in). In <i>A. r. vindhiana</i>, 80 eggs measured from 58 to 75.1&#160;mm (2.28 to 2.96&#160;in) by 46.4 to 57.6&#160;mm (1.83 to 2.27&#160;in), with an average of 66&#160;mm ×&#160;52.8&#160;mm (2.60&#160;in ×&#160;2.08&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_young_and_parental_behaviour">Development of young and parental behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Development of young and parental behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Eggs are <a href="/wiki/Egg_incubation" title="Egg incubation">incubated</a> by the female for 40–44 days, with extreme records of 30 to 45 days, before hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Incubation tends to begin with the first egg and may be done exclusively by the female in India but, in African data, the male sometimes briefly relieves here.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> When the nests are climbed up to, the females are tight sitters, often flying at the very last minute.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Green lining may still be added at the incubation stage.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Upon hatching, the young apparently have to be constantly brooded or shaded from strong sun in the very open nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The chicks are initially covered with white down, with a black bill, yellow cere and feet and brown eyes; a thicker white coat is acquired at 2 weeks and 1 week later the 1st feathers appear on scapulars and wing coverts.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The young eaglets can stand weakly at about 3 weeks, walk around the nest at 4 weeks and start to wing-flap about a week later.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Wing and tail quills sprout rapidly, with feathers appearing down the side of the breast at 4 weeks. By 5 weeks, feathers cover much of the body except for the head and underparts. By week 7, the chick has only a small amount of <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">down feathers</a> remaining and weighs around 2.15&#160;kg (4.7&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The rapid development of dorsal feathers is comparable to other raptors that use open nest sites such as <a href="/wiki/Snake_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Snake eagle">snake eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secretarybird" title="Secretarybird">secretarybirds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Only one chick usually survives after hatching.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_46-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> This is often due to <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">siblicide</a>, where the older chick fatally wounds the younger chick in the first few days of life. The older sibling weighs around 143&#160;g (5.0&#160;oz) while the younger one weighs about 85&#160;g (3.0&#160;oz) frequently at the point of its demise or disappearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In southern Africa, there are at least four cases of two fledglings occurring in one nest, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> At around 5 weeks, the eaglet adopts anti-predator behaviours, some laying prone while a novel animal approaches while others adopt a truculent threat posture with feathers raised, gape opened, wings poised to slap and talons barred for slashing.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> For the first 10 days, the adult female observes the chick very closely, relying on food provisioned by the male. After two weeks, the chick is left alone for 2.5 hours each day, whilst the adults forage. At this point the male may start making direct food deliveries to the eaglet. This is considered a relatively early point to stop attending to the nest for an eagle this size and the nest often soon becomes unsuitably foul with remains.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> One 39 day old eaglet was able to tear up its own food already but was still primarily fed by the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The first flight attempts are around 7-10 weeks but the chick is fully grown and capable of <a href="/wiki/Fledging" class="mw-redirect" title="Fledging">fledging</a> the nest fully after 10–12 weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>However, the female may remain to shelter during rainstorms around to as late as the fledging stage.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The full stage of dependence is ongoing for about 6 weeks after fledging. The young tawny eagle may stay with the parents even until next breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In India at least, after the nesting period, the pairs disperse and leave the nesting area, seldom being seen near the nest until pairing off again initiates in October.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A juvenile tawny eagle that was shot at 2 years old was 48&#160;km (30&#160;mi) away from its original nest while 2 juveniles at 5 months and 7 months old were 50 and 34&#160;km (31 and 21&#160;mi) away, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_11-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nesting_success_and_failures">Nesting success and failures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nesting success and failures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nest losses of eggs and young appears to be quite high.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Young eaglets often die, at times by their siblings, and if poorly guarded nests are often predated by a probably wide range of predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Nesting success is driven by quality of habitats and food access.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Breeding efforts in Zimbabwe produced 19 young in 26 pair years with a replacement rate of 0.73 young per pair per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In India, tawny eagles pairs seem to adapt to suboptimal overly sandy habitats by more dispersing nests, and can show similar productivity of chicks per nest as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In Hwange National Park, 72.4% of pairs present were thought to breed on average in the course of a year, with an average of 0.61 fledglings produced per effort.<sup id="cite_ref-Hustler3_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler3-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> This is and other studies support that rainfall is key to productive success in tawny eagles of this area, with far more two egg clutches rather than one egg ones (which usually failed) and less confined breeding periods in years that had greater rainfall.<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hustler3_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hustler3-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success, recorded as young per pair per year (ypy), was lower still in <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsavo_East_National_Park" title="Tsavo East National Park">Tsavo East National Park</a> than in <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> (0.4, 0.5 and 0.78 ypy respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-Steyn2_52-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Higher nesting success was found in Zambia, where pair produced a mean of 1 fledgling per nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne_107-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Although an extensive study of lifespan are not known to have taken place for the tawny eagle, it is known that these eagles can live up to at least 16 years of age in the wild.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Aquila_rapax.jpg/220px-Aquila_rapax.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Aquila_rapax.jpg/330px-Aquila_rapax.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Aquila_rapax.jpg/440px-Aquila_rapax.jpg 2x" data-file-width="693" data-file-height="481" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_rapax.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An unusually light juvenile tawny eagle from India.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The tawny eagle still occupies a large range.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In Africa, it has been estimated that the range of the species covers about 15 million square kilometers, in addition to a range of about 3.1 million square kilometers in Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As recently as the 1990s, the global population was thought to possibly range into six figures with a population in Asia at that time thought to be in the hundreds of thousands alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_3-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, the species is currently listed as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a> on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> list of Threatened species.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The current population is far less than half of what it was once thought to be, with only about 100,000 to just under 500,000 individuals thought to persist worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> There was a clear decrease in tawny eagle sightings between <a href="/wiki/SABAP" class="mw-redirect" title="SABAP">SABAP</a> and <a href="/wiki/SABAP2" title="SABAP2">SABAP2</a> in Southern Africa, occurring in only 323 of 1440 quarter degree grid cells.<sup id="cite_ref-Underhill_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Underhill-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> During close study of the tawny and martial eagle in central <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, a precipitous decline was detected in both, with a tawny eagle population that was once regionally numbered about 19 pairs down to 2 known pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-CJ2_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ2-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The once seemingly innumerous population of this species within <a href="/wiki/Kgalagadi_Transfrontier_Park" title="Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park">Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park</a> was known by the 1990s to be down to merely 40 known pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Roadside counts conducted in <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> show that although the majority of raptor species are in drastic population decline, only the tawny eagle and snake eagles are surviving outside of protected areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay3_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay3-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> In India, the tawny eagle was once considered “our commonest eagle”<sup id="cite_ref-AliS_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AliS-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> but strong declines have been detected with surveys indicating strongholds like <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> have shown reductions of observed pairs by up to half.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> According to the producer-scrounger foraging theory, vultures are to some extent reliant on tawny eagles to help locate carcasses.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogada_158-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogada-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Thus, the conservation of eagles outside protected areas is of vital importance to ensure the survival of vultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Ogada_158-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ogada-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Threats">Threats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Threats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg/220px-Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg/330px-Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg/440px-Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1696" data-file-height="2261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Serengeti_Raubadler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tawny eagle in the <a href="/wiki/Serengeti" title="Serengeti">Serengeti</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Tawny eagles face a number of threats that affect their breeding behaviour, foraging success and ultimately the survival of individual birds. The most recent and devastating threat to survival occurred on 20 June 2019. The <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carcasses</a> of 468 <a href="/wiki/White-backed_vulture" title="White-backed vulture">white-backed vultures</a>, 17 <a href="/wiki/White-headed_vulture" title="White-headed vulture">white-headed vultures</a>, 28 <a href="/wiki/Hooded_vulture" title="Hooded vulture">hooded vultures</a>, 14 <a href="/wiki/Lappet-faced_vulture" title="Lappet-faced vulture">lappet-faced vultures</a> and 10 <a href="/wiki/Cape_vulture" title="Cape vulture">cape vultures</a> were found alongside 2 tawny eagles.  A total of 537 <a href="/wiki/Vulture" title="Vulture">vultures</a> and 2 eagles were found poisoned in northern <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>. It is suspected that they died after eating the carcasses of 3 <a href="/wiki/African_bush_elephant" title="African bush elephant">elephants</a> that were laced with poison by poachers. Carcasses are poisoned to ensure that scavengers are unable to aid rangers in the effort to locate poached wildlife. By circling above dead animals, large raptors act as an early detection system for <a href="/wiki/Anti-poaching" title="Anti-poaching">anti-poaching</a> rangers.<sup id="cite_ref-NDTV_AFP_06-2019_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDTV_AFP_06-2019-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN_06-2019_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN_06-2019-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_06-2019_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian_06-2019-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Afrik21_06-2019_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Afrik21_06-2019-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Poisoning events are far from restricted to Botswana and are thought to be a direct factor in the reduction of tawny eagles as well even in the protected areas of <a href="/wiki/Kruger_National_Park" title="Kruger National Park">Kruger National Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> In central <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, all 5 of the juvenile tawny eagles that were radio-tagged were poisoned by strychnine baits, completely decimating all recruitment of the species in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-CJ2_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ2-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Mysteriously, the populations of bateleurs and tawny eagles in the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_Mara" title="Maasai Mara">Maasai Mara</a> appear to be bumping up as opposed to the declines reported elsewhere, seemingly in sync with the worsening declines of vultures on the Maasai.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further threats to tawny eagles include habitat loss and land-use changes such as intensified cattle grazing, firewood collection and sale and the charcoal industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Such culling of the spare trees of arid India seem to be the primary driver of less understand decline of tawny eagles in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_5-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A seemingly higher instance of <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> infections also seems to affecting the tawny eagles of India.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Raptor populations are reliant on seasonal rainfall events which influence the survival of prey populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> is alternating rainfall patterns in the arid regions of Southern Africa and impacting on prey populations. There is a clear correlation between rainfall events and breeding success of tawny eagles. It was found that the projected decline of tawny eagles from climate change, which is already underway, begun via impact population persistence, first effecting population dynamics, the composition of biological communities and finally biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann3_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann3-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Electrocutions and collision risks associated with <a href="/wiki/Overhead_power_line" title="Overhead power line">overhead power lines</a> remain a constant threat to large eagles and <a href="/wiki/Vultures" class="mw-redirect" title="Vultures">vultures</a>. Furthermore, the powerline nesting raptors were found to be a significant source of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_fault" title="Electrical fault">line faulting</a> in the area, causing substantial financial issues.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_191-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, tawny eagles are also killed by flying into various manmade objects such as <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoirs</a> or are <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">killed by automobiles</a> and are at risk at <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> in India.<sup id="cite_ref-CJ2_202-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ2-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The overarching threat to any raptor population is human population increase which causes competition for habitat and food resources.<sup id="cite_ref-Thiollay_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thiollay-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Key to conservation of the tawny eagle population is mitigating the effects of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Also, clearly, the banning of poison baits and the mitigation of dangerous powerlines in eagle-utilized areas is obviously key for the survival of the tawny eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-CJ2_202-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CJ2-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN_06-2019_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN_06-2019-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jenkins_191-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tawny_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
